   1	"My uncle's goodness is extreme,
   2	If seriously he hath disease;
   3	He hath acquired the world's esteem
   4	And nothing more important sees;
   5	A paragon of virtue he!
   6	But what a nuisance it will be,
   7	Chained to his bedside night and day
   8	Without a chance to slip away.
   9	Ye need dissimulation base
  10	A dying man with art to soothe,
  11	Beneath his head the pillow smooth,
  12	And physic bring with mournful face,
  13	To sigh and meditate alone:
  14	When will the devil take his own!"
  15	Thus mused a madcap young, who drove
  16	Through clouds of dust at postal pace,
  17	By the decree of Mighty Jove,
  18	Inheritor of all his race.
  19	Friends of Liudmila and Ruslan,
  20	Let me present ye to the man,
  21	Who without more prevarication
  22	The hero is of my narration!
  23	Oneguine, O my gentle readers,
  24	Was born beside the Neva, where
  25	It may be ye were born, or there
  26	Have shone as one of fashion's leaders.
  27	I also wandered there of old,
  28	But cannot stand the northern cold.
  29	Having performed his service truly,
  30	Deep into debt his father ran;
  31	Three balls a year he gave ye duly,
  32	At last became a ruined man.
  33	But Eugene was by fate preserved,
  34	For first "madame" his wants observed,
  35	And then "monsieur" supplied her place;
  36	The boy was wild but full of grace.
  37	"Monsieur l'Abbe" a starving Gaul,
  38	Fearing his pupil to annoy,
  39	Instructed jestingly the boy,
  40	Morality taught scarce at all;
  41	Gently for pranks he would reprove
  42	And in the Summer Garden rove.
  43	When youth's rebellious hour drew near
  44	And my Eugene the path must trace--
  45	The path of hope and tender fear--
  46	Monsieur clean out of doors they chase.
  47	Lo! my Oneguine free as air,
  48	Cropped in the latest style his hair,
  49	Dressed like a London dandy he
  50	The giddy world at last shall see.
  51	He wrote and spoke, so all allowed,
  52	In the French language perfectly,
  53	Danced the mazurka gracefully,
  54	Without the least constraint he bowed.
  55	What more's required? The world replies,
  56	He is a charming youth and wise.
  57	We all of us of education
  58	A something somehow have obtained,
  59	Thus, praised be God! a reputation
  60	With us is easily attained.
  61	Oneguine was--so many deemed,
  62	Pedantic although scholar like,
  63	In truth he had the happy trick
  64	Without constraint in conversation
  65	Of touching lightly every theme.
  66	Silent, oracular ye'd see him
  67	Amid a serious disputation,
  68	Then suddenly discharge a joke
  69	The ladies' laughter to provoke.
  70	Latin is just now not in vogue,
  71	But if the truth I must relate,
  72	Oneguine knew enough, the rogue
  73	A mild quotation to translate,
  74	A little Juvenal to spout,
  75	With "vale" finish off a note;
  76	Two verses he could recollect
  77	Of the AEneid, but incorrect.
  78	In history he took no pleasure,
  79	The dusty chronicles of earth
  80	For him were but of little worth,
  81	Yet still of anecdotes a treasure
  82	Within his memory there lay,
  83	From Romulus unto our day.
  84	For empty sound the rascal swore he
  85	Existence would not make a curse,
  86	Knew not an iamb from a choree,
  87	Although we read him heaps of verse.
  88	Homer, Theocritus, he jeered,
  89	But Adam Smith to read appeared,
  90	And at economy was great;
  91	That is, he could elucidate
  92	How empires store of wealth unfold,
  93	How flourish, why and wherefore less
  94	If the raw product they possess
  95	The medium is required of gold.
  96	The father scarcely understands
  97	His son and mortgages his lands.
  98	But upon all that Eugene knew
  99	I have no leisure here to dwell,
 100	But say he was a genius who
 101	In one thing really did excel.
 102	It occupied him from a boy,
 103	A labour, torment, yet a joy,
 104	It whiled his idle hours away
 105	And wholly occupied his day--
 106	The amatory science warm,
 107	Which Ovid once immortalized,
 108	For which the poet agonized
 109	Laid down his life of sun and storm
 110	On the steppes of Moldavia lone,
 111	Far from his Italy--his own.
 112	How soon he learnt deception's art,
 113	Hope to conceal and jealousy,
 114	False confidence or doubt to impart,
 115	Sombre or glad in turn to be,
 116	Haughty appear, subservient,
 117	Obsequious or indifferent!
 118	What languor would his silence show,
 119	How full of fire his speech would glow!
 120	How artless was the note which spoke
 121	Of love again, and yet again;
 122	How deftly could he transport feign!
 123	How bright and tender was his look,
 124	Modest yet daring! And a tear
 125	Would at the proper time appear.How well he played the greenhorn's part
 126	To cheat the inexperienced fair,
 127	Sometimes by pleasing flattery's art,
 128	Sometimes by ready-made despair;
 129	The feeble moment would espy
 130	Of tender years the modesty
 131	Conquer by passion and address,
 132	Await the long-delayed caress.
 133	Avowal then 'twas time to pray,
 134	Attentive to the heart's first beating,
 135	Follow up love--a secret meeting
 136	Arrange without the least delay--
 137	Then, then--well, in some solitude
 138	Lessons to give he understood!How soon he learnt to titillate
 139	The heart of the inveterate flirt!
 140	Desirous to annihilate
 141	His own antagonists expert,
 142	How bitterly he would malign,
 143	With many a snare their pathway line!
 144	But ye, O happy husbands, ye
 145	With him were friends eternally:
 146	The crafty spouse caressed him, who
 147	By Faublas in his youth was schooled,
 148	And the suspicious veteran old,
 149	The pompous, swaggering cuckold too,
 150	Who floats contentedly through life,
 151	Proud of his dinners and his wife!
 152	One morn whilst yet in bed he lay,
 153	His valet brings him letters three.
 154	What, invitations? The same day
 155	As many entertainments be!
 156	A ball here, there a children's treat,
 157	Whither shall my rapscallion flit?
 158	Whither shall he go first? He'll see,
 159	Perchance he will to all the three.
 160	Meantime in matutinal dress
 161	And hat surnamed a "Bolivar"
 162	He hies unto the "Boulevard,"
 163	To loiter there in idleness
 164	Until the sleepless Breguet chime
 165	Announcing to him dinner-time.
 166	'Tis dark. He seats him in a sleigh,
 167	"Drive on!" the cheerful cry goes forth,
 168	His furs are powdered on the way
 169	By the fine silver of the north.
 170	He bends his course to Talon's, where
 171	He knows Kaverine will repair.
 172	He enters. High the cork arose
 173	And Comet champagne foaming flows.
 174	Before him red roast beef is seen
 175	And truffles, dear to youthful eyes,
 176	Flanked by immortal Strasbourg pies,
 177	The choicest flowers of French cuisine,
 178	And Limburg cheese alive and old
 179	Is seen next pine-apples of gold.
 180	Still thirst fresh draughts of wine compels
 181	To cool the cutlets' seething grease,
 182	When the sonorous Breguet tells
 183	Of the commencement of the piece.
 184	A critic of the stage malicious,
 185	A slave of actresses capricious,
 186	Oneguine was a citizen
 187	Of the domains of the side-scene.
 188	To the theatre he repairs
 189	Where each young critic ready stands,
 190	Capers applauds with clap of hands,
 191	With hisses Cleopatra scares,
 192	Moina recalls for this alone
 193	That all may hear his voice's tone.Thou fairy-land! Where formerly
 194	Shone pungent Satire's dauntless king,
 195	Von Wisine, friend of liberty,
 196	And Kniajnine, apt at copying.
 197	The young Simeonova too there
 198	With Ozeroff was wont to share
 199	Applause, the people's donative.
 200	There our Katenine did revive
 201	Corneille's majestic genius,
 202	Sarcastic Shakhovskoi brought out
 203	His comedies, a noisy rout,
 204	There Didelot became glorious,
 205	There, there, beneath the side-scene's shade
 206	The drama of my youth was played.
 207	My goddesses, where are your shades?
 208	Do ye not hear my mournful sighs?
 209	Are ye replaced by other maids
 210	Who cannot conjure former joys?
 211	Shall I your chorus hear anew,
 212	Russia's Terpsichore review
 213	Again in her ethereal dance?
 214	Or will my melancholy glance
 215	On the dull stage find all things changed,
 216	The disenchanted glass direct
 217	Where I can no more recollect?--
 218	A careless looker-on estranged
 219	In silence shall I sit and yawn
 220	And dream of life's delightful dawn?The house is crammed. A thousand lamps
 221	On pit, stalls, boxes, brightly blaze,
 222	Impatiently the gallery stamps,
 223	The curtain now they slowly raise.
 224	Obedient to the magic strings,
 225	Brilliant, ethereal, there springs
 226	Forth from the crowd of nymphs surrounding
 227	Istomina the nimbly-bounding;
 228	With one foot resting on its tip
 229	Slow circling round its fellow swings
 230	And now she skips and now she springs
 231	Like down from Aeolus's lip,
 232	Now her lithe form she arches o'er
 233	And beats with rapid foot the floor.
 234	Shouts of applause! Oneguine passes
 235	Between the stalls, along the toes;
 236	Seated, a curious look with glasses
 237	On unknown female forms he throws.
 238	Free scope he yields unto his glance,
 239	Reviews both dress and countenance,
 240	With all dissatisfaction shows.
 241	To male acquaintances he bows,
 242	And finally he deigns let fall
 243	Upon the stage his weary glance.
 244	He yawns, averts his countenance,
 245	Exclaiming, "We must change 'em all!
 246	I long by ballets have been bored,
 247	Now Didelot scarce can be endured!"Snakes, satyrs, loves with many a shout
 248	Across the stage still madly sweep,
 249	Whilst the tired serving-men without
 250	Wrapped in their sheepskins soundly sleep.
 251	Still the loud stamping doth not cease,
 252	Still they blow noses, cough, and sneeze,
 253	Still everywhere, without, within,
 254	The lamps illuminating shine;
 255	The steed benumbed still pawing stands
 256	And of the irksome harness tires,
 257	And still the coachmen round the fires
 258	Abuse their masters, rub their hands:
 259	But Eugene long hath left the press
 260	To array himself in evening dress.
 261	Faithfully shall I now depict,
 262	Portray the solitary den
 263	Wherein the child of fashion strict
 264	Dressed him, undressed, and dressed again?
 265	All that industrial London brings
 266	For tallow, wood and other things
 267	Across the Baltic's salt sea waves,
 268	All which caprice and affluence craves,
 269	All which in Paris eager taste,
 270	Choosing a profitable trade,
 271	For our amusement ever made
 272	And ease and fashionable waste,--
 273	Adorned the apartment of Eugene,
 274	Philosopher just turned eighteen.China and bronze the tables weight,
 275	Amber on pipes from Stamboul glows,
 276	And, joy of souls effeminate,
 277	Phials of crystal scents enclose.
 278	Combs of all sizes, files of steel,
 279	Scissors both straight and curved as well,
 280	Of thirty different sorts, lo! brushes
 281	Both for the nails and for the tushes.
 282	Rousseau, I would remark in passing,
 283	Could not conceive how serious Grimm
 284	Dared calmly cleanse his nails 'fore him,
 285	Eloquent raver all-surpassing,--
 286	The friend of liberty and laws
 287	In this case quite mistaken was.
 288	The most industrious man alive
 289	May yet be studious of his nails;
 290	What boots it with the age to strive?
 291	Custom the despot soon prevails.
 292	A new Kaverine Eugene mine,
 293	Dreading the world's remarks malign,
 294	Was that which we are wont to call
 295	A fop, in dress pedantical.
 296	Three mortal hours per diem he
 297	Would loiter by the looking-glass,
 298	And from his dressing-room would pass
 299	Like Venus when, capriciously,
 300	The goddess would a masquerade
 301	Attend in male attire arrayed.On this artistical retreat
 302	Having once fixed your interest,
 303	I might to connoisseurs repeat
 304	The style in which my hero dressed;
 305	Though I confess I hardly dare
 306	Describe in detail the affair,
 307	Since words like pantaloons, vest, coat,
 308	To Russ indigenous are not;
 309	And also that my feeble verse--
 310	Pardon I ask for such a sin--
 311	With words of foreign origin
 312	Too much I'm given to intersperse,
 313	Though to the Academy I come
 314	And oft its Dictionary thumb.
 315	But such is not my project now,
 316	So let us to the ball-room haste,
 317	Whither at headlong speed doth go
 318	Eugene in hackney carriage placed.
 319	Past darkened windows and long streets
 320	Of slumbering citizens he fleets,
 321	Till carriage lamps, a double row,
 322	Cast a gay lustre on the snow,
 323	Which shines with iridescent hues.
 324	He nears a spacious mansion's gate,
 325	By many a lamp illuminate,
 326	And through the lofty windows views
 327	Profiles of lovely dames he knows
 328	And also fashionable beaux.Our hero stops and doth alight,
 329	Flies past the porter to the stair,
 330	But, ere he mounts the marble flight,
 331	With hurried hand smooths down his hair.
 332	He enters: in the hall a crowd,
 333	No more the music thunders loud,
 334	Some a mazurka occupies,
 335	Crushing and a confusing noise;
 336	Spurs of the Cavalier Guard clash,
 337	The feet of graceful ladies fly,
 338	And following them ye might espy
 339	Full many a glance like lightning flash,
 340	And by the fiddle's rushing sound
 341	The voice of jealousy is drowned.In my young days of wild delight
 342	On balls I madly used to dote,
 343	Fond declarations they invite
 344	Or the delivery of a note.
 345	So hearken, every worthy spouse,
 346	I would your vigilance arouse,
 347	Attentive be unto my rhymes
 348	And due precautions take betimes.
 349	Ye mothers also, caution use,
 350	Upon your daughters keep an eye,
 351	Employ your glasses constantly,
 352	For otherwise--God only knows!
 353	I lift a warning voice because
 354	I long have ceased to offend the laws.Alas! life's hours which swiftly fly
 355	I've wasted in amusements vain,
 356	But were it not immoral I
 357	Should dearly like a dance again.
 358	I love its furious delight,
 359	The crowd and merriment and light,
 360	The ladies, their fantastic dress,
 361	Also their feet--yet ne'ertheless
 362	Scarcely in Russia can ye find
 363	Three pairs of handsome female feet;
 364	Ah! I still struggle to forget
 365	A pair; though desolate my mind,
 366	Their memory lingers still and seems
 367	To agitate me in my dreams.When, where, and in what desert land,
 368	Madman, wilt thou from memory raze
 369	Those feet? Alas! on what far strand
 370	Do ye of spring the blossoms graze?
 371	Lapped in your Eastern luxury,
 372	No trace ye left in passing by
 373	Upon the dreary northern snows,
 374	But better loved the soft repose
 375	Of splendid carpets richly wrought.
 376	I once forgot for your sweet cause
 377	The thirst for fame and man's applause,
 378	My country and an exile's lot;
 379	My joy in youth was fleeting e'en
 380	As your light footprints on the green.Diana's bosom, Flora's cheeks,
 381	Are admirable, my dear friend,
 382	But yet Terpsichore bespeaks
 383	Charms more enduring in the end.
 384	For promises her feet reveal
 385	Of untold gain she must conceal,
 386	Their privileged allurements fire
 387	A hidden train of wild desire.
 388	I love them, O my dear Elvine,
 389	Beneath the table-cloth of white,
 390	In winter on the fender bright,
 391	In springtime on the meadows green,
 392	Upon the ball-room's glassy floor
 393	Or by the ocean's rocky shore.
 394	Beside the stormy sea one day
 395	I envied sore the billows tall,
 396	Which rushed in eager dense array
 397	Enamoured at her feet to fall.
 398	How like the billow I desired
 399	To kiss the feet which I admired!
 400	No, never in the early blaze
 401	Of fiery youth's untutored days
 402	So ardently did I desire
 403	A young Armida's lips to press,
 404	Her cheek of rosy loveliness
 405	Or bosom full of languid fire,--
 406	A gust of passion never tore
 407	My spirit with such pangs before.Another time, so willed it Fate,
 408	Immersed in secret thought I stand
 409	And grasp a stirrup fortunate--
 410	Her foot was in my other hand.
 411	Again imagination blazed,
 412	The contact of the foot I raised
 413	Rekindled in my withered heart
 414	The fires of passion and its smart--
 415	Away! and cease to ring their praise
 416	For ever with thy tattling lyre,
 417	The proud ones are not worth the fire
 418	Of passion they so often raise.
 419	The words and looks of charmers sweet
 420	Are oft deceptive--like their feet.Where is Oneguine? Half asleep,
 421	Straight from the ball to bed he goes,
 422	Whilst Petersburg from slumber deep
 423	The drum already doth arouse.
 424	The shopman and the pedlar rise
 425	And to the Bourse the cabman plies;
 426	The Okhtenka with pitcher speeds,
 427	Crunching the morning snow she treads;
 428	Morning awakes with joyous sound;
 429	The shutters open; to the skies
 430	In column blue the smoke doth rise;
 431	The German baker looks around
 432	His shop, a night-cap on his head,
 433	And pauses oft to serve out bread.
 434	But turning morning into night,
 435	Tired by the ball's incessant noise,
 436	The votary of vain delight
 437	Sleep in the shadowy couch enjoys,
 438	Late in the afternoon to rise,
 439	When the same life before him lies
 440	Till morn--life uniform but gay,
 441	To-morrow just like yesterday.
 442	But was our friend Eugene content,
 443	Free, in the blossom of his spring,
 444	Amidst successes flattering
 445	And pleasure's daily blandishment,
 446	Or vainly 'mid luxurious fare
 447	Was he in health and void of care?--Even so! His passions soon abated,
 448	Hateful the hollow world became,
 449	Nor long his mind was agitated
 450	By love's inevitable flame.
 451	For treachery had done its worst;
 452	Friendship and friends he likewise curst,
 453	Because he could not gourmandise
 454	Daily beefsteaks and Strasbourg pies
 455	And irrigate them with champagne;
 456	Nor slander viciously could spread
 457	Whene'er he had an aching head;
 458	And, though a plucky scatterbrain,
 459	He finally lost all delight
 460	In bullets, sabres, and in fight.His malady, whose cause I ween
 461	It now to investigate is time,
 462	Was nothing but the British spleen
 463	Transported to our Russian clime.
 464	It gradually possessed his mind;
 465	Though, God be praised! he ne'er designed
 466	To slay himself with blade or ball,
 467	Indifferent he became to all,
 468	And like Childe Harold gloomily
 469	He to the festival repairs,
 470	Nor boston nor the world's affairs
 471	Nor tender glance nor amorous sigh
 472	Impressed him in the least degree,--
 473	Callous to all he seemed to be.Ye miracles of courtly grace,
 474	He left _you_ first, and I must own
 475	The manners of the highest class
 476	Have latterly vexatious grown;
 477	And though perchance a lady may
 478	Discourse of Bentham or of Say,
 479	Yet as a rule their talk I call
 480	Harmless, but quite nonsensical.
 481	Then they're so innocent of vice,
 482	So full of piety, correct,
 483	So prudent, and so circumspect
 484	Stately, devoid of prejudice,
 485	So inaccessible to men,
 486	Their looks alone produce the spleen.
 487	And you, my youthful damsels fair,
 488	Whom latterly one often meets
 489	Urging your droshkies swift as air
 490	Along Saint Petersburg's paved streets,
 491	From you too Eugene took to flight,
 492	Abandoning insane delight,
 493	And isolated from all men,
 494	Yawning betook him to a pen.
 495	He thought to write, but labour long
 496	Inspired him with disgust and so
 497	Nought from his pen did ever flow,
 498	And thus he never fell among
 499	That vicious set whom I don't blame--
 500	Because a member I became.Once more to idleness consigned,
 501	He felt the laudable desire
 502	From mere vacuity of mind
 503	The wit of others to acquire.
 504	A case of books he doth obtain--
 505	He reads at random, reads in vain.
 506	This nonsense, that dishonest seems,
 507	This wicked, that absurd he deems,
 508	All are constrained and fetters bear,
 509	Antiquity no pleasure gave,
 510	The moderns of the ancients rave--
 511	Books he abandoned like the fair,
 512	His book-shelf instantly doth drape
 513	With taffety instead of crape.Having abjured the haunts of men,
 514	Like him renouncing vanity,
 515	His friendship I acquired just then;
 516	His character attracted me.
 517	An innate love of meditation,
 518	Original imagination,
 519	And cool sagacious mind he had:
 520	I was incensed and he was sad.
 521	Both were of passion satiate
 522	And both of dull existence tired,
 523	Extinct the flame which once had fired;
 524	Both were expectant of the hate
 525	With which blind Fortune oft betrays
 526	The very morning of our days.He who hath lived and living, thinks,
 527	Must e'en despise his kind at last;
 528	He who hath suffered ofttimes shrinks
 529	From shades of the relentless past.
 530	No fond illusions live to soothe,
 531	But memory like a serpent's tooth
 532	With late repentance gnaws and stings.
 533	All this in many cases brings
 534	A charm with it in conversation.
 535	Oneguine's speeches I abhorred
 536	At first, but soon became inured
 537	To the sarcastic observation,
 538	To witticisms and taunts half-vicious
 539	And gloomy epigrams malicious.How oft, when on a summer night
 540	Transparent o'er the Neva beamed
 541	The firmament in mellow light,
 542	And when the watery mirror gleamed
 543	No more with pale Diana's rays,
 544	We called to mind our youthful days--
 545	The days of love and of romance!
 546	Then would we muse as in a trance,
 547	Impressionable for an hour,
 548	And breathe the balmy breath of night;
 549	And like the prisoner's our delight
 550	Who for the greenwood quits his tower,
 551	As on the rapid wings of thought
 552	The early days of life we sought.
 553	Absorbed in melancholy mood
 554	And o'er the granite coping bent,
 555	Oneguine meditative stood,
 556	E'en as the poet says he leant.
 557	'Tis silent all! Alone the cries
 558	Of the night sentinels arise
 559	And from the Millionaya afar
 560	The sudden rattling of a car.
 561	Lo! on the sleeping river borne,
 562	A boat with splashing oar floats by,
 563	And now we hear delightedly
 564	A jolly song and distant horn;
 565	But sweeter in a midnight dream
 566	Torquato Tasso's strains I deem.
 567	Ye billows of blue Hadria's sea,
 568	O Brenta, once more we shall meet
 569	And, inspiration firing me,
 570	Your magic voices I shall greet,
 571	Whose tones Apollo's sons inspire,
 572	And after Albion's proud lyre
 573	Possess my love and sympathy.
 574	The nights of golden Italy
 575	I'll pass beneath the firmament,
 576	Hid in the gondola's dark shade,
 577	Alone with my Venetian maid,
 578	Now talkative, now reticent;
 579	From her my lips shall learn the tongue
 580	Of love which whilom Petrarch sung.
 581	When will my hour of freedom come!
 582	Time, I invoke thee! favouring gales
 583	Awaiting on the shore I roam
 584	And beckon to the passing sails.
 585	Upon the highway of the sea
 586	When shall I wing my passage free
 587	On waves by tempests curdled o'er!
 588	'Tis time to quit this weary shore
 589	So uncongenial to my mind,
 590	To dream upon the sunny strand
 591	Of Africa, ancestral land,
 592	Of dreary Russia left behind,
 593	Wherein I felt love's fatal dart,
 594	Wherein I buried left my heart.
 595	Eugene designed with me to start
 596	And visit many a foreign clime,
 597	But Fortune cast our lots apart
 598	For a protracted space of time.
 599	Just at that time his father died,
 600	And soon Oneguine's door beside
 601	Of creditors a hungry rout
 602	Their claims and explanations shout.
 603	But Eugene, hating litigation
 604	And with his lot in life content,
 605	To a surrender gave consent,
 606	Seeing in this no deprivation,
 607	Or counting on his uncle's death
 608	And what the old man might bequeath.And in reality one day
 609	The steward sent a note to tell
 610	How sick to death his uncle lay
 611	And wished to say to him farewell.
 612	Having this mournful document
 613	Perused, Eugene in postchaise went
 614	And hastened to his uncle's side,
 615	But in his heart dissatisfied,
 616	Having for money's sake alone
 617	Sorrow to counterfeit and wail--
 618	Thus we began our little tale--
 619	But, to his uncle's mansion flown,
 620	He found him on the table laid,
 621	A due which must to earth be paid.The courtyard full of serfs he sees,
 622	And from the country all around
 623	Had come both friends and enemies--
 624	Funeral amateurs abound!
 625	The body they consigned to rest,
 626	And then made merry pope and guest,
 627	With serious air then went away
 628	As men who much had done that day.
 629	Lo! my Oneguine rural lord!
 630	Of mines and meadows, woods and lakes,
 631	He now a full possession takes,
 632	He who economy abhorred,
 633	Delighted much his former ways
 634	To vary for a few brief days.For two whole days it seemed a change
 635	To wander through the meadows still,
 636	The cool dark oaken grove to range,
 637	To listen to the rippling rill.
 638	But on the third of grove and mead
 639	He took no more the slightest heed;
 640	They made him feel inclined to doze;
 641	And the conviction soon arose,
 642	Ennui can in the country dwell
 643	Though without palaces and streets,
 644	Cards, balls, routs, poetry or fetes;
 645	On him spleen mounted sentinel
 646	And like his shadow dogged his life,
 647	Or better,--like a faithful wife.I was for calm existence made,
 648	For rural solitude and dreams,
 649	My lyre sings sweeter in the shade
 650	And more imagination teems.
 651	On innocent delights I dote,
 652	Upon my lake I love to float,
 653	For law I _far niente_ take
 654	And every morning I awake
 655	The child of sloth and liberty.
 656	I slumber much, a little read,
 657	Of fleeting glory take no heed.
 658	In former years thus did not I
 659	In idleness and tranquil joy
 660	The happiest days of life employ?Love, flowers, the country, idleness
 661	And fields my joys have ever been;
 662	I like the difference to express
 663	Between myself and my Eugene,
 664	Lest the malicious reader or
 665	Some one or other editor
 666	Of keen sarcastic intellect
 667	Herein my portrait should detect,
 668	And impiously should declare,
 669	To sketch myself that I have tried
 670	Like Byron, bard of scorn and pride,
 671	As if impossible it were
 672	To write of any other elf
 673	Than one's own fascinating self.Here I remark all poets are
 674	Love to idealize inclined;
 675	I have dreamed many a vision fair
 676	And the recesses of my mind
 677	Retained the image, though short-lived,
 678	Which afterwards the muse revived.
 679	Thus carelessly I once portrayed
 680	Mine own ideal, the mountain maid,
 681	The captives of the Salguir's shore.
 682	But now a question in this wise
 683	Oft upon friendly lips doth rise:
 684	Whom doth thy plaintive Muse adore?
 685	To whom amongst the jealous throng
 686	Of maids dost thou inscribe thy song?
 687	Whose glance reflecting inspiration
 688	With tenderness hath recognized
 689	Thy meditative incantation--
 690	Whom hath thy strain immortalized?
 691	None, be my witness Heaven above!
 692	The malady of hopeless love
 693	I have endured without respite.
 694	Happy who thereto can unite
 695	Poetic transport. They impart
 696	A double force unto their song
 697	Who following Petrarch move along
 698	And ease the tortures of the heart--
 699	Perchance they laurels also cull--
 700	But I, in love, was mute and dull.The Muse appeared, when love passed by
 701	And my dark soul to light was brought;
 702	Free, I renewed the idolatry
 703	Of harmony enshrining thought.
 704	I write, and anguish flies away,
 705	Nor doth my absent pen portray
 706	Around my stanzas incomplete
 707	Young ladies' faces and their feet.
 708	Extinguished ashes do not blaze--
 709	I mourn, but tears I cannot shed--
 710	Soon, of the tempest which hath fled
 711	Time will the ravages efface--
 712	When that time comes, a poem I'll strive
 713	To write in cantos twenty-five.I've thought well o'er the general plan,
 714	The hero's name too in advance,
 715	Meantime I'll finish whilst I can
 716	Canto the First of this romance.
 717	I've scanned it with a jealous eye,
 718	Discovered much absurdity,
 719	But will not modify a tittle--
 720	I owe the censorship a little.
 721	For journalistic deglutition
 722	I yield the fruit of work severe.
 723	Go, on the Neva's bank appear,
 724	My very latest composition!
 725	Enjoy the meed which Fame bestows--
 726	Misunderstanding, words and blows.
 727	The PoetCanto The SecondThe village wherein yawned Eugene
 728	Was a delightful little spot,
 729	There friends of pure delight had been
 730	Grateful to Heaven for their lot.
 731	The lonely mansion-house to screen
 732	From gales a hill behind was seen;
 733	Before it ran a stream. Behold!
 734	Afar, where clothed in green and gold
 735	Meadows and cornfields are displayed,
 736	Villages in the distance show
 737	And herds of oxen wandering low;
 738	Whilst nearer, sunk in deeper shade,
 739	A thick immense neglected grove
 740	Extended--haunt which Dryads love.
 741	'Twas built, the venerable pile,
 742	As lordly mansions ought to be,
 743	In solid, unpretentious style,
 744	The style of wise antiquity.
 745	Lofty the chambers one and all,
 746	Silk tapestry upon the wall,
 747	Imperial portraits hang around
 748	And stoves of various shapes abound.
 749	All this I know is out of date,
 750	I cannot tell the reason why,
 751	But Eugene, incontestably,
 752	The matter did not agitate,
 753	Because he yawned at the bare view
 754	Of drawing-rooms or old or new.
 755	He took the room wherein the old
 756	Man--forty years long in this wise--
 757	His housekeeper was wont to scold,
 758	Look through the window and kill flies.
 759	'Twas plain--an oaken floor ye scan,
 760	Two cupboards, table, soft divan,
 761	And not a speck of dirt descried.
 762	Oneguine oped the cupboards wide.
 763	In one he doth accounts behold,
 764	Here bottles stand in close array,
 765	There jars of cider block the way,
 766	An almanac but eight years old.
 767	His uncle, busy man indeed,
 768	No other book had time to read.
 769	Alone amid possessions great,
 770	Eugene at first began to dream,
 771	If but to lighten Time's dull rate,
 772	Of many an economic scheme;
 773	This anchorite amid his waste
 774	The ancient _barshtchina_ replaced
 775	By an _obrok's_ indulgent rate:
 776	The peasant blessed his happy fate.
 777	But this a heinous crime appeared
 778	Unto his neighbour, man of thrift,
 779	Who secretly denounced the gift,
 780	And many another slily sneered;
 781	And all with one accord agreed,
 782	He was a dangerous fool indeed.
 783	All visited him at first, of course;
 784	But since to the backdoor they led
 785	Most usually a Cossack horse
 786	Upon the Don's broad pastures bred
 787	If they but heard domestic loads
 788	Come rumbling up the neighbouring roads,
 789	Most by this circumstance offended
 790	All overtures of friendship ended.
 791	"Oh! what a fool our neighbour is!
 792	He's a freemason, so we think.
 793	Alone he doth his claret drink,
 794	A lady's hand doth never kiss.
 795	'Tis _yes! no!_ never _madam! sir!_"
 796	This was his social character.
 797	Into the district then to boot
 798	A new proprietor arrived,
 799	From whose analysis minute
 800	The neighbourhood fresh sport derived.
 801	Vladimir Lenski was his name,
 802	From Gottingen inspired he came,
 803	A worshipper of Kant, a bard,
 804	A young and handsome galliard.
 805	He brought from mystic Germany
 806	The fruits of learning and combined
 807	A fiery and eccentric mind,
 808	Idolatry of liberty,
 809	A wild enthusiastic tongue,
 810	Black curls which to his shoulders hung.
 811	The pervert world with icy chill
 812	Had not yet withered his young breast.
 813	His heart reciprocated still
 814	When Friendship smiled or Love caressed.
 815	He was a dear delightful fool--
 816	A nursling yet for Hope to school.
 817	The riot of the world and glare
 818	Still sovereigns of his spirit were,
 819	And by a sweet delusion he
 820	Would soothe the doubtings of his soul,
 821	He deemed of human life the goal
 822	To be a charming mystery:
 823	He racked his brains to find its clue
 824	And marvels deemed he thus should view.
 825	This he believed: a kindred spirit
 826	Impelled to union with his own
 827	Lay languishing both day and night--
 828	Waiting his coming--his alone!
 829	He deemed his friends but longed to make
 830	Great sacrifices for his sake!
 831	That a friend's arm in every case
 832	Felled a calumniator base!
 833	That chosen heroes consecrate,
 834	Friends of the sons of every land,
 835	Exist--that their immortal band
 836	Shall surely, be it soon or late,
 837	Pour on this orb a dazzling light
 838	And bless mankind with full delight.
 839	Compassion now or wrath inspires
 840	And now philanthropy his soul,
 841	And now his youthful heart desires
 842	The path which leads to glory's goal.
 843	His harp beneath that sky had rung
 844	Where sometime Goethe, Schiller sung,
 845	And at the altar of their fame
 846	He kindled his poetic flame.
 847	But from the Muses' loftiest height
 848	The gifted songster never swerved,
 849	But proudly in his song preserved
 850	An ever transcendental flight;
 851	His transports were quite maidenly,
 852	Charming with grave simplicity.He sang of love--to love a slave.
 853	His ditties were as pure and bright
 854	As thoughts which gentle maidens have,
 855	As a babe's slumber, or the light
 856	Of the moon in the tranquil skies,
 857	Goddess of lovers' tender sighs.
 858	He sang of separation grim,
 859	Of what not, and of distant dim,
 860	Of roses to romancers dear;
 861	To foreign lands he would allude,
 862	Where long time he in solitude
 863	Had let fall many a bitter tear:
 864	He sang of life's fresh colours stained
 865	Before he eighteen years attained.Since Eugene in that solitude
 866	Gifts such as these alone could prize,
 867	A scant attendance Lenski showed
 868	At neighbouring hospitalities.
 869	He shunned those parties boisterous;
 870	The conversation tedious
 871	About the crop of hay, the wine,
 872	The kennel or a kindred line,
 873	Was certainly not erudite
 874	Nor sparkled with poetic fire,
 875	Nor wit, nor did the same inspire
 876	A sense of social delight,
 877	But still more stupid did appear
 878	The gossip of their ladies fair.Handsome and rich, the neighbourhood
 879	Lenski as a good match received,--
 880	Such is the country custom good;
 881	All mothers their sweet girls believed
 882	Suitable for this semi-Russian.
 883	He enters: rapidly discussion
 884	Shifts, tacks about, until they prate
 885	The sorrows of a single state.
 886	Perchance where Dunia pours out tea
 887	The young proprietor we find;
 888	To Dunia then they whisper: Mind!
 889	And a guitar produced we see,
 890	And Heavens! warbled forth we hear:
 891	_Come to my golden palace, dear_!
 892	But Lenski, having no desire
 893	Vows matrimonial to break,
 894	With our Oneguine doth aspire
 895	Acquaintance instantly to make.
 896	They met. Earth, water, prose and verse,
 897	Or ice and flame, are not diverse
 898	If they were similar in aught.
 899	At first such contradictions wrought
 900	Mutual repulsion and ennui,
 901	But grown familiar side by side
 902	On horseback every day they ride--
 903	Inseparable soon they be.
 904	Thus oft--this I myself confess--
 905	Men become friends from idleness.But even thus not now-a-days!
 906	In spite of common sense we're wont
 907	As cyphers others to appraise,
 908	Ourselves as unities to count;
 909	And like Napoleons each of us
 910	A million bipeds reckons thus
 911	One instrument for his own use--
 912	Feeling is silly, dangerous.
 913	Eugene, more tolerant than this
 914	(Though certainly mankind he knew
 915	And usually despised it too),
 916	Exceptionless as no rule is,
 917	A few of different temper deemed,
 918	Feeling in others much esteemed.With smiling face he Lenski hears;
 919	The poet's fervid conversation
 920	And judgment which unsteady veers
 921	And eye which gleams with inspiration--
 922	All this was novel to Eugene.
 923	The cold reply with gloomy mien
 924	He oft upon his lips would curb,
 925	Thinking: 'tis foolish to disturb
 926	This evanescent boyish bliss.
 927	Time without me will lessons give,
 928	So meantime let him joyous live
 929	And deem the world perfection is!
 930	Forgive the fever youth inspires,
 931	And youthful madness, youthful fires.The gulf between them was so vast,
 932	Debate commanded ample food--
 933	The laws of generations past,
 934	The fruits of science, evil, good,
 935	The prejudices all men have,
 936	The fatal secrets of the grave,
 937	And life and fate in turn selected
 938	Were to analysis subjected.
 939	The fervid poet would recite,
 940	Carried away by ecstasy,
 941	Fragments of northern poetry,
 942	Whilst Eugene condescending quite,
 943	Though scarcely following what was said,
 944	Attentive listened to the lad.But more the passions occupy
 945	The converse of our hermits twain,
 946	And, heaving a regretful sigh,
 947	An exile from their troublous reign,
 948	Eugene would speak regarding these.
 949	Thrice happy who their agonies
 950	Hath suffered but indifferent grown,
 951	Still happier he who ne'er hath known!
 952	By absence who hath chilled his love,
 953	His hate by slander, and who spends
 954	Existence without wife or friends,
 955	Whom jealous transport cannot move,
 956	And who the rent-roll of his race
 957	Ne'er trusted to the treacherous ace.When, wise at length, we seek repose
 958	Beneath the flag of Quietude,
 959	When Passion's fire no longer glows
 960	And when her violence reviewed--
 961	Each gust of temper, silly word,
 962	Seems so unnatural and absurd:
 963	Reduced with effort unto sense,
 964	We hear with interest intense
 965	The accents wild of other's woes,
 966	They stir the heart as heretofore.
 967	So ancient warriors, battles o'er,
 968	A curious interest disclose
 969	In yarns of youthful troopers gay,
 970	Lost in the hamlet far away.And in addition youth is flame
 971	And cannot anything conceal,
 972	Is ever ready to proclaim
 973	The love, hate, sorrow, joy, we feel.
 974	Deeming himself a veteran scarred
 975	In love's campaigns Oneguine heard
 976	With quite a lachrymose expression
 977	The youthful poet's fond confession.
 978	He with an innocence extreme
 979	His inner consciousness laid bare,
 980	And Eugene soon discovered there
 981	The story of his young love's dream,
 982	Where plentifully feelings flow
 983	Which we experienced long ago.Alas! he loved as in our times
 984	Men love no more, as only the
 985	Mad spirit of the man who rhymes
 986	Is still condemned in love to be;
 987	One image occupied his mind,
 988	Constant affection intertwined
 989	And an habitual sense of pain;
 990	And distance interposed in vain,
 991	Nor years of separation all
 992	Nor homage which the Muse demands
 993	Nor beauties of far distant lands
 994	Nor study, banquet, rout nor ball
 995	His constant soul could ever tire,
 996	Which glowed with virginal desire.When but a boy he Olga loved
 997	Unknown as yet the aching heart,
 998	He witnessed tenderly and moved
 999	Her girlish gaiety and sport.
1000	Beneath the sheltering oak tree's shade
1001	He with his little maiden played,
1002	Whilst the fond parents, friends thro' life,
1003	Dreamed in the future man and wife.
1004	And full of innocent delight,
1005	As in a thicket's humble shade,
1006	Beneath her parents' eyes the maid
1007	Grew like a lily pure and white,
1008	Unseen in thick and tangled grass
1009	By bee and butterfly which pass.'Twas she who first within his breast
1010	Poetic transport did infuse,
1011	And thoughts of Olga first impressed
1012	A mournful temper on his Muse.
1013	Farewell! thou golden days of love!
1014	'Twas then he loved the tangled grove
1015	And solitude and calm delight,
1016	The moon, the stars, and shining night--
1017	The moon, the lamp of heaven above,
1018	To whom we used to consecrate
1019	A promenade in twilight late
1020	With tears which secret sufferers love--
1021	But now in her effulgence pale
1022	A substitute for lamps we hail!Obedient she had ever been
1023	And modest, cheerful as the morn,
1024	As a poetic life serene,
1025	Sweet as the kiss of lovers sworn.
1026	Her eyes were of cerulean blue,
1027	Her locks were of a golden hue,
1028	Her movements, voice and figure slight,
1029	All about Olga--to a light
1030	Romance of love I pray refer,
1031	You'll find her portrait there, I vouch;
1032	I formerly admired her much
1033	But finally grew bored by her.
1034	But with her elder sister I
1035	Must now my stanzas occupy.Tattiana was her appellation.
1036	We are the first who such a name
1037	In pages of a love narration
1038	With such a perversity proclaim.
1039	But wherefore not?--'Tis pleasant, nice,
1040	Euphonious, though I know a spice
1041	It carries of antiquity
1042	And of the attic. Honestly,
1043	We must admit but little taste
1044	Doth in us or our names appear,
1045	And education runs to waste,
1046	Endowing us from out her store
1047	With affectation,--nothing more.
1048	And so Tattiana was her name,
1049	Nor by her sister's brilliancy
1050	Nor by her beauty she became
1051	The cynosure of every eye.
1052	Shy, silent did the maid appear
1053	As in the timid forest deer,
1054	Even beneath her parents' roof
1055	Stood as estranged from all aloof,
1056	Nearest and dearest knew not how
1057	To fawn upon and love express;
1058	A child devoid of childishness
1059	To romp and play she ne'er would go:
1060	Oft staring through the window pane
1061	Would she in silence long remain.Contemplativeness, her delight,
1062	E'en from her cradle's earliest dream,
1063	Adorned with many a vision bright
1064	Of rural life the sluggish stream;
1065	Ne'er touched her fingers indolent
1066	The needle nor, o'er framework bent,
1067	Would she the canvas tight enrich
1068	With gay design and silken stitch.
1069	Desire to rule ye may observe
1070	When the obedient doll in sport
1071	An infant maiden doth exhort
1072	Polite demeanour to preserve,
1073	Gravely repeating to another
1074	Recent instructions of its mother.But Tania ne'er displayed a passion
1075	For dolls, e'en from her earliest years,
1076	And gossip of the town and fashion
1077	She ne'er repeated unto hers.
1078	Strange unto her each childish game,
1079	But when the winter season came
1080	And dark and drear the evenings were,
1081	Terrible tales she loved to hear.
1082	And when for Olga nurse arrayed
1083	In the broad meadow a gay rout,
1084	All the young people round about,
1085	At prisoner's base she never played.
1086	Their noisy laugh her soul annoyed,
1087	Their giddy sports she ne'er enjoyed.She loved upon the balcony
1088	To anticipate the break of day,
1089	When on the pallid eastern sky
1090	The starry beacons fade away,
1091	The horizon luminous doth grow,
1092	Morning's forerunners, breezes blow
1093	And gradually day unfolds.
1094	In winter, when Night longer holds
1095	A hemisphere beneath her sway,
1096	Longer the East inert reclines
1097	Beneath the moon which dimly shines,
1098	And calmly sleeps the hours away,
1099	At the same hour she oped her eyes
1100	And would by candlelight arise.Romances pleased her from the first,
1101	Her all in all did constitute;
1102	In love adventures she was versed,
1103	Rousseau and Richardson to boot.
1104	Not a bad fellow was her father
1105	Though superannuated rather;
1106	In books he saw nought to condemn
1107	But, as he never opened them,
1108	Viewed them with not a little scorn,
1109	And gave himself but little pain
1110	His daughter's book to ascertain
1111	Which 'neath her pillow lay till morn.
1112	His wife was also mad upon
1113	The works of Mr. Richardson.She was thus fond of Richardson
1114	Not that she had his works perused,
1115	Or that adoring Grandison
1116	That rascal Lovelace she abused;
1117	But that Princess Pauline of old,
1118	Her Moscow cousin, often told
1119	The tale of these romantic men;
1120	Her husband was a bridegroom then,
1121	And she despite herself would waste
1122	Sighs on another than her lord
1123	Whose qualities appeared to afford
1124	More satisfaction to her taste.
1125	Her Grandison was in the Guard,
1126	A noted fop who gambled hard.Like his, her dress was always nice,
1127	The height of fashion, fitting tight,
1128	But contrary to her advice
1129	The girl in marriage they unite.
1130	Then, her distraction to allay,
1131	The bridegroom sage without delay
1132	Removed her to his country seat,
1133	Where God alone knows whom she met.
1134	She struggled hard at first thus pent,
1135	Night separated from her spouse,
1136	Then became busy with the house,
1137	First reconciled and then content;
1138	Habit was given us in distress
1139	By Heaven in lieu of happiness.Habit alleviates the grief
1140	Inseparable from our lot;
1141	This great discovery relief
1142	And consolation soon begot.
1143	And then she soon 'twixt work and leisure
1144	Found out the secret how at pleasure
1145	To dominate her worthy lord,
1146	And harmony was soon restored.
1147	The workpeople she superintended,
1148	Mushrooms for winter salted down,
1149	Kept the accounts, shaved many a crown,
1150	The bath on Saturdays attended,
1151	When angry beat her maids, I grieve,
1152	And all without her husband's leave.
1153	In her friends' albums, time had been,
1154	With blood instead of ink she scrawled,
1155	Baptized Prascovia Pauline,
1156	And in her conversation drawled.
1157	She wore her corset tightly bound,
1158	The Russian N with nasal sound
1159	She would pronounce _a la Francaise_;
1160	But soon she altered all her ways,
1161	Corset and album and Pauline,
1162	Her sentimental verses all,
1163	She soon forgot, began to call
1164	Akulka who was once Celine,
1165	And had with waddling in the end
1166	Her caps and night-dresses to mend.As for her spouse he loved her dearly,
1167	In her affairs ne'er interfered,
1168	Entrusted all to her sincerely,
1169	In dressing-gown at meals appeared.
1170	Existence calmly sped along,
1171	And oft at eventide a throng
1172	Of friends unceremonious would
1173	Assemble from the neighbourhood:
1174	They growl a bit--they scandalise--
1175	They crack a feeble joke and smile--
1176	Thus the time passes and meanwhile
1177	Olga the tea must supervise--
1178	'Tis time for supper, now for bed,
1179	And soon the friendly troop hath fled.They in a peaceful life preserved
1180	Customs by ages sanctified,
1181	Strictly the Carnival observed,
1182	Ate Russian pancakes at Shrovetide,
1183	Twice in the year to fast were bound,
1184	Of whirligigs were very fond,
1185	Of Christmas carols, song and dance;
1186	When people with long countenance
1187	On Trinity Sunday yawned at prayer,
1188	Three tears they dropt with humble mein
1189	Upon a bunch of lovage green;
1190	_Kvass_ needful was to them as air;
1191	On guests their servants used to wait
1192	By rank as settled by the State.
1193	Thus age approached, the common doom,
1194	And death before the husband wide
1195	Opened the portals of the tomb
1196	And a new diadem supplied.
1197	Just before dinner-time he slept,
1198	By neighbouring families bewept,
1199	By children and by faithful wife
1200	With deeper woe than others' grief.
1201	He was an honest gentleman,
1202	And where at last his bones repose
1203	The epitaph on marble shows:
1204	_Demetrius Larine, sinful man,
1205	Servant of God and brigadier,
1206	Enjoyeth peaceful slumber here_.
1207	To his Penates now returned,
1208	Vladimir Lenski visited
1209	His neighbour's lowly tomb and mourned
1210	Above the ashes of the dead.
1211	There long time sad at heart he stayed:
1212	"Poor Yorick," mournfully he said,
1213	"How often in thine arms I lay;
1214	How with thy medal I would play,
1215	The Medal Otchakoff conferred!
1216	To me he would his Olga give,
1217	Would whisper: shall I so long live?"--
1218	And by a genuine sorrow stirred,
1219	Lenski his pencil-case took out
1220	And an elegiac poem wrote.
1221	Likewise an epitaph with tears
1222	He writes upon his parents' tomb,
1223	And thus ancestral dust reveres.
1224	Oh! on the fields of life how bloom
1225	Harvests of souls unceasingly
1226	By Providence's dark decree!
1227	They blossom, ripen and they fall
1228	And others rise ephemeral!
1229	Thus our light race grows up and lives,
1230	A moment effervescing stirs,
1231	Then seeks ancestral sepulchres,
1232	The appointed hour arrives, arrives!
1233	And our successors soon shall drive
1234	Us from the world wherein we live.Meantime, drink deeply of the flow
1235	Of frivolous existence, friends;
1236	Its insignificance I know
1237	And care but little for its ends.
1238	To dreams I long have closed mine eyes,
1239	Yet sometimes banished hopes will rise
1240	And agitate my heart again;
1241	And thus it is 'twould cause me pain
1242	Without the faintest trace to leave
1243	This world. I do not praise desire,
1244	Yet still apparently aspire
1245	My mournful fate in verse to weave,
1246	That like a friendly voice its tone
1247	Rescue me from oblivion.Perchance some heart 'twill agitate,
1248	And then the stanzas of my theme
1249	Will not, preserved by kindly Fate,
1250	Perish absorbed by Lethe's stream.
1251	Then it may be, O flattering tale,
1252	Some future ignoramus shall
1253	My famous portrait indicate
1254	And cry: he was a poet great!
1255	My gratitude do not disdain,
1256	Admirer of the peaceful Muse,
1257	Whose memory doth not refuse
1258	My light productions to retain,
1259	Whose hands indulgently caress
1260	The bays of age and helplessness.
1261	The Country DamselCanto The Third"Whither away? Deuce take the bard!"--
1262	"Good-bye, Oneguine, I must go."--
1263	"I won't detain you; but 'tis hard
1264	To guess how you the eve pull through."--
1265	"At Larina's."--"Hem, that is queer!
1266	Pray is it not a tough affair
1267	Thus to assassinate the eve?"--
1268	"Not at all."--"That I can't conceive!
1269	'Tis something of this sort I deem.
1270	In the first place, say, am I right?
1271	A Russian household simple quite,
1272	Who welcome guests with zeal extreme,
1273	Preserves and an eternal prattle
1274	About the rain and flax and cattle."--
1275	"No misery I see in that"--
1276	"Boredom, my friend, behold the ill--"
1277	"Your fashionable world I hate,
1278	Domestic life attracts me still,
1279	Where--"--"What! another eclogue spin?
1280	For God's sake, Lenski, don't begin!
1281	What! really going? 'Tis too bad!
1282	But Lenski, I should be so glad
1283	Would you to me this Phyllis show,
1284	Fair source of every fine idea,
1285	Verses and tears et cetera.
1286	Present me."--"You are joking."--"No."--
1287	"Delighted."--"When?"--"This very night.
1288	They will receive us with delight."
1289	Whilst homeward by the nearest route
1290	Our heroes at full gallop sped,
1291	Can we not stealthily make out
1292	What they in conversation said?--
1293	"How now, Oneguine, yawning still?"--
1294	"'Tis habit, Lenski."--"Is your ill
1295	More troublesome than usual?"--"No!
1296	How dark the night is getting though!
1297	Hallo, Andriushka, onward race!
1298	The drive becomes monotonous--
1299	Well! Larina appears to us
1300	An ancient lady full of grace.--
1301	That bilberry wine, I'm sore afraid,
1302	The deuce with my inside has played."
1303	"Say, of the two which was Tattiana?"
1304	"She who with melancholy face
1305	And silent as the maid Svetlana
1306	Hard by the window took her place."--
1307	"The younger, you're in love with her!"
1308	"Well!"--"I the elder should prefer,
1309	Were I like you a bard by trade--
1310	In Olga's face no life's displayed.
1311	'Tis a Madonna of Vandyk,
1312	An oval countenance and pink,
1313	Yon silly moon upon the brink
1314	Of the horizon she is like!"--
1315	Vladimir something curtly said
1316	Nor further comment that night made.
1317	Meantime Oneguine's apparition
1318	At Larina's abode produced
1319	Quite a sensation; the position
1320	To all good neighbours' sport conduced.
1321	Endless conjectures all propound
1322	And secretly their views expound.
1323	What jokes and guesses now abound,
1324	A beau is for Tattiana found!
1325	In fact, some people were assured
1326	The wedding-day had been arranged,
1327	But the date subsequently changed
1328	Till proper rings could be procured.
1329	On Lenski's matrimonial fate
1330	They long ago had held debate.
1331	Of course Tattiana was annoyed
1332	By such allusions scandalous,
1333	Yet was her inmost soul o'erjoyed
1334	With satisfaction marvellous,
1335	As in her heart the thought sank home,
1336	I am in love, my hour hath come!
1337	Thus in the earth the seed expands
1338	Obedient to warm Spring's commands.
1339	Long time her young imagination
1340	By indolence and languor fired
1341	The fated nutriment desired;
1342	And long internal agitation
1343	Had filled her youthful breast with gloom,
1344	She waited for--I don't know whom!
1345	The fatal hour had come at last--
1346	She oped her eyes and cried: 'tis he!
1347	Alas! for now before her passed
1348	The same warm vision constantly;
1349	Now all things round about repeat
1350	Ceaselessly to the maiden sweet
1351	His name: the tenderness of home
1352	Tiresome unto her hath become
1353	And the kind-hearted servitors:
1354	Immersed in melancholy thought,
1355	She hears of conversation nought
1356	And hated casual visitors,
1357	Their coming which no man expects,
1358	And stay whose length none recollects.
1359	Now with what eager interest
1360	She the delicious novel reads,
1361	With what avidity and zest
1362	She drinks in those seductive deeds!
1363	All the creations which below
1364	From happy inspiration flow,
1365	The swain of Julia Wolmar,
1366	Malek Adel and De Linar,
1367	Werther, rebellious martyr bold,
1368	And that unrivalled paragon,
1369	The sleep-compelling Grandison,
1370	Our tender dreamer had enrolled
1371	A single being: 'twas in fine
1372	No other than Oneguine mine.
1373	Dreaming herself the heroine
1374	Of the romances she preferred,
1375	Clarissa, Julia, Delphine,--
1376	Tattiana through the forest erred,
1377	And the bad book accompanies.
1378	Upon those pages she descries
1379	Her passion's faithful counterpart,
1380	Fruit of the yearnings of the heart.
1381	She heaves a sigh and deep intent
1382	On raptures, sorrows not her own,
1383	She murmurs in an undertone
1384	A letter for her hero meant:
1385	That hero, though his merit shone,
1386	Was certainly no Grandison.
1387	Alas! my friends, the years flit by
1388	And after them at headlong pace
1389	The evanescent fashions fly
1390	In motley and amusing chase.
1391	The world is ever altering!
1392	Farthingales, patches, were the thing,
1393	And courtier, fop, and usurer
1394	Would once in powdered wig appear;
1395	Time was, the poet's tender quill
1396	In hopes of everlasting fame
1397	A finished madrigal would frame
1398	Or couplets more ingenious still;
1399	Time was, a valiant general might
1400	Serve who could neither read nor write.Time was, in style magniloquent
1401	Authors replete with sacred fire
1402	Their heroes used to represent
1403	All that perfection could desire;
1404	Ever by adverse fate oppressed,
1405	Their idols they were wont to invest
1406	With intellect, a taste refined,
1407	And handsome countenance combined,
1408	A heart wherein pure passion burnt;
1409	The excited hero in a trice
1410	Was ready for self-sacrifice,
1411	And in the final tome we learnt,
1412	Vice had due punishment awarded,
1413	Virtue was with a bride rewarded.But now our minds are mystified
1414	And Virtue acts as a narcotic,
1415	Vice in romance is glorified
1416	And triumphs in career erotic.
1417	The monsters of the British Muse
1418	Deprive our schoolgirls of repose,
1419	The idols of their adoration
1420	A Vampire fond of meditation,
1421	Or Melmoth, gloomy wanderer he,
1422	The Eternal Jew or the Corsair
1423	Or the mysterious Sbogar.
1424	Byron's capricious phantasy
1425	Could in romantic mantle drape
1426	E'en hopeless egoism's dark shape.
1427	My friends, what means this odd digression?
1428	May be that I by heaven's decrees
1429	Shall abdicate the bard's profession,
1430	And shall adopt some new caprice.
1431	Thus having braved Apollo's rage
1432	With humble prose I'll fill my page
1433	And a romance in ancient style
1434	Shall my declining years beguile;
1435	Nor shall my pen paint terribly
1436	The torment born of crime unseen,
1437	But shall depict the touching scene
1438	Of Russian domesticity;
1439	I will descant on love's sweet dream,
1440	The olden time shall be my theme.Old people's simple conversations
1441	My unpretending page shall fill,
1442	Their offspring's innocent flirtations
1443	By the old lime-tree or the rill,
1444	Their Jealousy and separation
1445	And tears of reconciliation:
1446	Fresh cause of quarrel then I'll find,
1447	But finally in wedlock bind.
1448	The passionate speeches I'll repeat,
1449	Accents of rapture or despair
1450	I uttered to my lady fair
1451	Long ago, prostrate at her feet.
1452	Then they came easily enow,
1453	My tongue is somewhat rusty now.Tattiana! sweet Tattiana, see!
1454	What bitter tears with thee I shed!
1455	Thou hast resigned thy destiny
1456	Unto a ruthless tyrant dread.
1457	Thou'lt suffer, dearest, but before,
1458	Hope with her fascinating power
1459	To dire contentment shall give birth
1460	And thou shalt taste the joys of earth.
1461	Thou'lt quaff love's sweet envenomed stream,
1462	Fantastic images shall swarm
1463	In thy imagination warm,
1464	Of happy meetings thou shalt dream,
1465	And wheresoe'er thy footsteps err,
1466	Confront thy fated torturer!Love's pangs Tattiana agonize.
1467	She seeks the garden in her need--
1468	Sudden she stops, casts down her eyes
1469	And cares not farther to proceed;
1470	Her bosom heaves whilst crimson hues
1471	With sudden flush her cheeks suffuse,
1472	Barely to draw her breath she seems,
1473	Her eye with fire unwonted gleams.
1474	And now 'tis night, the guardian moon
1475	Sails her allotted course on high,
1476	And from the misty woodland nigh
1477	The nightingale trills forth her tune;
1478	Restless Tattiana sleepless lay
1479	And thus unto her nurse did say:"Nurse, 'tis so close I cannot rest.
1480	Open the window--sit by me."
1481	"What ails thee, dear?"--"I feel depressed.
1482	Relate some ancient history."
1483	"But which, my dear?--In days of yore
1484	Within my memory I bore
1485	Many an ancient legend which
1486	In monsters and fair dames was rich;
1487	But now my mind is desolate,
1488	What once I knew is clean forgot--
1489	Alas! how wretched now my lot!"
1490	"But tell me, nurse, can you relate
1491	The days which to your youth belong?
1492	Were you in love when you were young?"--"Alack! Tattiana," she replied,
1493	"We never loved in days of old,
1494	My mother-in-law who lately died
1495	Had killed me had the like been told."
1496	"How came you then to wed a man?"--
1497	"Why, as God ordered! My Ivan
1498	Was younger than myself, my light,
1499	For I myself was thirteen quite;
1500	The matchmaker a fortnight sped,
1501	Her suit before my parents pressing:
1502	At last my father gave his blessing,
1503	And bitter tears of fright I shed.
1504	Weeping they loosed my tresses long
1505	And led me off to church with song."
1506	"Then amongst strangers I was left--
1507	But I perceive thou dost not heed--"
1508	"Alas! dear nurse, my heart is cleft,
1509	Mortally sick I am indeed.
1510	Behold, my sobs I scarce restrain--"
1511	"My darling child, thou art in pain.--
1512	The Lord deliver her and save!
1513	Tell me at once what wilt thou have?
1514	I'll sprinkle thee with holy water.--
1515	How thy hands burn!"--"Dear nurse, I'm well.
1516	I am--in love--you know--don't tell!"
1517	"The Lord be with thee, O my daughter!"--
1518	And the old nurse a brief prayer said
1519	And crossed with trembling hand the maid."I am in love," her whispers tell
1520	The aged woman in her woe:
1521	"My heart's delight, thou art not well."--
1522	"I am in love, nurse! leave me now."
1523	Behold! the moon was shining bright
1524	And showed with an uncertain light
1525	Tattiana's beauty, pale with care,
1526	Her tears and her dishevelled hair;
1527	And on the footstool sitting down
1528	Beside our youthful heroine fair,
1529	A kerchief round her silver hair
1530	The aged nurse in ample gown,
1531	Whilst all creation seemed to dream
1532	Enchanted by the moon's pale beam.
1533	But borne in spirit far away
1534	Tattiana gazes on the moon,
1535	And starting suddenly doth say:
1536	"Nurse, leave me. I would be alone.
1537	Pen, paper bring: the table too
1538	Draw near. I soon to sleep shall go--
1539	Good-night." Behold! she is alone!
1540	'Tis silent--on her shines the moon--
1541	Upon her elbow she reclines,
1542	And Eugene ever in her soul
1543	Indites an inconsiderate scroll
1544	Wherein love innocently pines.
1545	Now it is ready to be sent--
1546	For whom, Tattiana, is it meant?I have known beauties cold and raw
1547	As Winter in their purity,
1548	Striking the intellect with awe
1549	By dull insensibility,
1550	And I admired their common sense
1551	And natural benevolence,
1552	But, I acknowledge, from them fled;
1553	For on their brows I trembling read
1554	The inscription o'er the gates of Hell
1555	"Abandon hope for ever here!"
1556	Love to inspire doth woe appear
1557	To such--delightful to repel.
1558	Perchance upon the Neva e'en
1559	Similar dames ye may have seen.
1560	Amid submissive herds of men
1561	Virgins miraculous I see,
1562	Who selfishly unmoved remain
1563	Alike by sighs and flattery.
1564	But what astonished do I find
1565	When harsh demeanour hath consigned
1566	A timid love to banishment?--
1567	On fresh allurements they are bent,
1568	At least by show of sympathy;
1569	At least their accents and their words
1570	Appear attuned to softer chords;
1571	And then with blind credulity
1572	The youthful lover once again
1573	Pursues phantasmagoria vain.Why is Tattiana guiltier deemed?--
1574	Because in singleness of thought
1575	She never of deception dreamed
1576	But trusted the ideal she wrought?--
1577	Because her passion wanted art,
1578	Obeyed the impulses of heart?--
1579	Because she was so innocent,
1580	That Heaven her character had blent
1581	With an imagination wild,
1582	With intellect and strong volition
1583	And a determined disposition,
1584	An ardent heart and yet so mild?--
1585	Doth love's incautiousness in her
1586	So irremissible appear?O ye whom tender love hath pained
1587	Without the ken of parents both,
1588	Whose hearts responsive have remained
1589	To the impressions of our youth,
1590	The all-entrancing joys of love--
1591	Young ladies, if ye ever strove
1592	The mystic lines to tear away
1593	A lover's letter might convey,
1594	Or into bold hands anxiously
1595	Have e'er a precious tress consigned,
1596	Or even, silent and resigned,
1597	When separation's hour drew nigh,
1598	Have felt love's agitated kiss
1599	With tears, confused emotions, bliss,--With unanimity complete,
1600	Condemn not weak Tattiana mine;
1601	Do not cold-bloodedly repeat
1602	The sneers of critics superfine;
1603	And you, O maids immaculate,
1604	Whom vice, if named, doth agitate
1605	E'en as the presence of a snake,
1606	I the same admonition make.
1607	Who knows? with love's consuming flame
1608	Perchance you also soon may burn,
1609	Then to some gallant in your turn
1610	Will be ascribed by treacherous Fame
1611	The triumph of a conquest new.
1612	The God of Love is after you!A coquette loves by calculation,
1613	Tattiana's love was quite sincere,
1614	A love which knew no limitation,
1615	Even as the love of children dear.
1616	She did not think "procrastination
1617	Enhances love in estimation
1618	And thus secures the prey we seek.
1619	His vanity first let us pique
1620	With hope and then perplexity,
1621	Excruciate the heart and late
1622	With jealous fire resuscitate,
1623	Lest jaded with satiety,
1624	The artful prisoner should seek
1625	Incessantly his chains to break."I still a complication view,
1626	My country's honour and repute
1627	Demands that I translate for you
1628	The letter which Tattiana wrote.
1629	At Russ she was by no means clever
1630	And read our newspapers scarce ever,
1631	And in her native language she
1632	Possessed nor ease nor fluency,
1633	So she in French herself expressed.
1634	I cannot help it I declare,
1635	Though hitherto a lady ne'er
1636	In Russ her love made manifest,
1637	And never hath our language proud
1638	In correspondence been allowed.
1639	They wish that ladies should, I hear,
1640	Learn Russian, but the Lord defend!
1641	I can't conceive a little dear
1642	With the "Well-Wisher" in her hand!
1643	I ask, all ye who poets are,
1644	Is it not true? the objects fair,
1645	To whom ye for unnumbered crimes
1646	Had to compose in secret rhymes,
1647	To whom your hearts were consecrate,--
1648	Did they not all the Russian tongue
1649	With little knowledge and that wrong
1650	In charming fashion mutilate?
1651	Did not their lips with foreign speech
1652	The native Russian tongue impeach?
1653	God grant I meet not at a ball
1654	Or at a promenade mayhap,
1655	A schoolmaster in yellow shawl
1656	Or a professor in tulle cap.
1657	As rosy lips without a smile,
1658	The Russian language I deem vile
1659	Without grammatical mistakes.
1660	May be, and this my terror wakes,
1661	The fair of the next generation,
1662	As every journal now entreats,
1663	Will teach grammatical conceits,
1664	Introduce verse in conversation.
1665	But I--what is all this to me?
1666	Will to the old times faithful be.Speech careless, incorrect, but soft,
1667	With inexact pronunciation
1668	Raises within my breast as oft
1669	As formerly much agitation.
1670	Repentance wields not now her spell
1671	And gallicisms I love as well
1672	As the sins of my youthful days
1673	Or Bogdanovitch's sweet lays.
1674	But I must now employ my Muse
1675	With the epistle of my fair;
1676	I promised!--Did I so?--Well, there!
1677	Now I am ready to refuse.
1678	I know that Parny's tender pen
1679	Is no more cherished amongst men.
1680	Bard of the "Feasts," and mournful breast,
1681	If thou wert sitting by my side,
1682	With this immoderate request
1683	I should alarm our friendship tried:
1684	In one of thine enchanting lays
1685	To russify the foreign phrase
1686	Of my impassioned heroine.
1687	Where art thou? Come! pretensions mine
1688	I yield with a low reverence;
1689	But lonely beneath Finnish skies
1690	Where melancholy rocks arise
1691	He wanders in his indolence;
1692	Careless of fame his spirit high
1693	Hears not my importunity!
1694	Tattiana's letter I possess,
1695	I guard it as a holy thing,
1696	And though I read it with distress,
1697	I'm o'er it ever pondering.
1698	Inspired by whom this tenderness,
1699	This gentle daring who could guess?
1700	Who this soft nonsense could impart,
1701	Imprudent prattle of the heart,
1702	Attractive in its banefulness?
1703	I cannot understand. But lo!
1704	A feeble version read below,
1705	A print without the picture's grace,
1706	Or, as it were, the Freischutz' score
1707	Strummed by a timid schoolgirl o'er.I write to you! Is more required?
1708	Can lower depths beyond remain?
1709	'Tis in your power now, if desired,
1710	To crush me with a just disdain.
1711	But if my lot unfortunate
1712	You in the least commiserate
1713	You will not all abandon me.
1714	At first, I clung to secrecy:
1715	Believe me, of my present shame
1716	You never would have heard the name,
1717	If the fond hope I could have fanned
1718	At times, if only once a week,
1719	To see you by our fireside stand,
1720	To listen to the words you speak,
1721	Address to you one single phrase
1722	And then to meditate for days
1723	Of one thing till again we met.
1724	'Tis said you are a misanthrope,
1725	In country solitude you mope,
1726	And we--an unattractive set--
1727	Can hearty welcome give alone.
1728	Why did you visit our poor place?
1729	Forgotten in the village lone,
1730	I never should have seen your face
1731	And bitter torment never known.
1732	The untutored spirit's pangs calmed down
1733	By time
1734	I had found my predestinate,
1735	Become a faithful wife and e'en
1736	A fond and careful mother been.
1737	Another! to none other I
1738	My heart's allegiance can resign,
1739	My doom has been pronounced on high,
1740	'Tis Heaven's will and I am thine.
1741	The sum of my existence gone
1742	But promise of our meeting gave,
1743	I feel thou wast by God sent down
1744	My guardian angel to the grave.
1745	Thou didst to me in dreams appear,
1746	Unseen thou wast already dear.
1747	Thine eye subdued me with strange glance,
1748	I heard thy voice's resonance
1749	Long ago. Dream it cannot be!
1750	Scarce hadst thou entered thee I knew,
1751	I flushed up, stupefied I grew,
1752	And cried within myself: 'tis he!
1753	Is it not truth? in tones suppressed
1754	With thee I conversed when I bore
1755	Comfort and succour to the poor,
1756	And when I prayer to Heaven addressed
1757	To ease the anguish of my breast.
1758	Nay! even as this instant fled,
1759	Was it not thou, O vision bright,
1760	That glimmered through the radiant night
1761	And gently hovered o'er my head?
1762	Was it not thou who thus didst stoop
1763	To whisper comfort, love and hope?
1764	Who art thou? Guardian angel sent
1765	Or torturer malevolent?
1766	Doubt and uncertainty decide:
1767	All this may be an empty dream,
1768	Delusions of a mind untried,
1769	Providence otherwise may deem--
1770	Then be it so! My destiny
1771	From henceforth I confide to thee!
1772	Lo! at thy feet my tears I pour
1773	And thy protection I implore.
1774	Imagine! Here alone am I!
1775	No one my anguish comprehends,
1776	At times my reason almost bends,
1777	And silently I here must die--
1778	But I await thee: scarce alive
1779	My heart with but one look revive;
1780	Or to disturb my dreams approach
1781	Alas! with merited reproach.
1782	'Tis finished. Horrible to read!
1783	With shame I shudder and with dread--
1784	But boldly I myself resign:
1785	Thine honour is my countersign!Tattiana moans and now she sighs
1786	And in her grasp the letter shakes,
1787	Even the rosy wafer dries
1788	Upon her tongue which fever bakes.
1789	Her head upon her breast declines
1790	And an enchanting shoulder shines
1791	From her half-open vest of night.
1792	But lo! already the moon's light
1793	Is waning. Yonder valley deep
1794	Looms gray behind the mist and morn
1795	Silvers the brook; the shepherd's horn
1796	Arouses rustics from their sleep.
1797	'Tis day, the family downstairs,
1798	But nought for this Tattiana cares.The break of day she doth not see,
1799	But sits in bed with air depressed,
1800	Nor on the letter yet hath she
1801	The image of her seal impressed.
1802	But gray Phillippevna the door
1803	Opened with care, and entering bore
1804	A cup of tea upon a tray.
1805	"'Tis time, my child, arise, I pray!
1806	My beauty, thou art ready too.
1807	My morning birdie, yesternight
1808	I was half silly with affright.
1809	But praised be God! in health art thou!
1810	The pains of night have wholly fled,
1811	Thy cheek is as a poppy red!""Ah! nurse, a favour do for me!"--
1812	"Command me, darling, what you choose"--
1813	"Do not--you might--suspicious be;
1814	But look you--ah! do not refuse."
1815	"I call to witness God on high--"
1816	"Then send your grandson quietly
1817	To take this letter to O-- Well!
1818	Unto our neighbour. Mind you tell--
1819	Command him not to say a word--
1820	I mean my name not to repeat."
1821	"To whom is it to go, my sweet?
1822	Of late I have been quite absurd,--
1823	So many neighbours here exist--
1824	Am I to go through the whole list?""How dull you are this morning, nurse!"
1825	"My darling, growing old am I!
1826	In age the memory gets worse,
1827	But I was sharp in times gone by.
1828	In times gone by thy bare command--"
1829	"Oh! nurse, nurse, you don't understand!
1830	What is thy cleverness to me?
1831	The letter is the thing, you see,--
1832	Oneguine's letter!"--"Ah! the thing!
1833	Now don't be cross with me, my soul,
1834	You know that I am now a fool--
1835	But why are your cheeks whitening?"
1836	"Nothing, good nurse, there's nothing wrong,
1837	But send your grandson before long."No answer all that day was borne.
1838	Another passed; 'twas just the same.
1839	Pale as a ghost and dressed since morn
1840	Tattiana waits. No answer came!
1841	Olga's admirer came that day:
1842	"Tell me, why doth your comrade stay?"
1843	The hostess doth interrogate:
1844	"He hath neglected us of late."--
1845	Tattiana blushed, her heart beat quick--
1846	"He promised here this day to ride,"
1847	Lenski unto the dame replied,
1848	"The post hath kept him, it is like."
1849	Shamefaced, Tattiana downward looked
1850	As if he cruelly had joked!'Twas dusk! Upon the table bright
1851	Shrill sang the _samovar_ at eve,
1852	The china teapot too ye might
1853	In clouds of steam above perceive.
1854	Into the cups already sped
1855	By Olga's hand distributed
1856	The fragrant tea in darkling stream,
1857	And a boy handed round the cream.
1858	Tania doth by the casement linger
1859	And breathes upon the chilly glass,
1860	Dreaming of what not, pretty lass,
1861	And traces with a slender finger
1862	Upon its damp opacity,
1863	The mystic monogram, O. E.
1864	In the meantime her spirit sinks,
1865	Her weary eyes are filled with tears--
1866	A horse's hoofs she hears--She shrinks!
1867	Nearer they come--Eugene appears!
1868	Ah! than a spectre from the dead
1869	More swift the room Tattiana fled,
1870	From hall to yard and garden flies,
1871	Not daring to cast back her eyes.
1872	She fears and like an arrow rushes
1873	Through park and meadow, wood and brake,
1874	The bridge and alley to the lake,
1875	Brambles she snaps and lilacs crushes,
1876	The flowerbeds skirts, the brook doth meet,
1877	Till out of breath upon a seatShe sank.--
1878	"He's here! Eugene is here!
1879	Merciful God, what will he deem?"
1880	Yet still her heart, which torments tear,
1881	Guards fondly hope's uncertain dream.
1882	She waits, on fire her trembling frame--
1883	Will he pursue?--But no one came.
1884	She heard of servant-maids the note,
1885	Who in the orchards gathered fruit,
1886	Singing in chorus all the while.
1887	(This by command; for it was found,
1888	However cherries might abound,
1889	They disappeared by stealth and guile,
1890	So mouths they stopt with song, not fruit--
1891	Device of rural minds acute!)Young maidens, fair maidens,
1892	Friends and companions,
1893	Disport yourselves, maidens,
1894	Arouse yourselves, fair ones.
1895	Come sing we in chorus
1896	The secrets of maidens.
1897	Allure the young gallant
1898	With dance and with song.
1899	As we lure the young gallant,
1900	Espy him approaching,
1901	Disperse yourselves, darlings,
1902	And pelt him with cherries,
1903	With cherries, red currants,
1904	With raspberries, cherries.
1905	Approach not to hearken
1906	To secrets of virgins,
1907	Approach not to gaze at
1908	The frolics of maidens.They sang, whilst negligently seated,
1909	Attentive to the echoing sound,
1910	Tattiana with impatience waited
1911	Until her heart less high should bound--
1912	Till the fire in her cheek decreased;
1913	But tremor still her frame possessed,
1914	Nor did her blushes fade away,
1915	More crimson every moment they.
1916	Thus shines the wretched butterfly,
1917	With iridescent wing doth flap
1918	When captured in a schoolboy's cap;
1919	Thus shakes the hare when suddenly
1920	She from the winter corn espies
1921	A sportsman who in covert lies.But finally she heaves a sigh,
1922	And rising from her bench proceeds;
1923	But scarce had turned the corner nigh,
1924	Which to the neighbouring alley leads,
1925	When Eugene like a ghost did rise
1926	Before her straight with roguish eyes.
1927	Tattiana faltered, and became
1928	Scarlet as burnt by inward flame.
1929	But this adventure's consequence
1930	To-day, my friends, at any rate,
1931	I am not strong enough to state;
1932	I, after so much eloquence,
1933	Must take a walk and rest a bit--
1934	Some day I'll somehow finish it.
1935	Rural LifeCanto The FourthThe less we love a lady fair
1936	The easier 'tis to gain her grace,
1937	And the more surely we ensnare
1938	Her in the pitfalls which we place.
1939	Time was when cold seduction strove
1940	To swagger as the art of love,
1941	Everywhere trumpeting its feats,
1942	Not seeking love but sensual sweets.
1943	But this amusement delicate
1944	Was worthy of that old baboon,
1945	Our fathers used to dote upon;
1946	The Lovelaces are out of date,
1947	Their glory with their heels of red
1948	And long perukes hath vanished.
1949	For who imposture can endure,
1950	A constant harping on one tune,
1951	Serious endeavours to assure
1952	What everybody long has known;
1953	Ever to hear the same replies
1954	And overcome antipathies
1955	Which never have existed, e'en
1956	In little maidens of thirteen?
1957	And what like menaces fatigues,
1958	Entreaties, oaths, fictitious fear,
1959	Epistles of six sheets or near,
1960	Rings, tears, deceptions and intrigues,
1961	Aunts, mothers and their scrutiny,
1962	And husbands' tedious amity?
1963	Such were the musings of Eugene.
1964	He in the early years of life
1965	Had a deluded victim been
1966	Of error and the passions' strife.
1967	By daily life deteriorated,
1968	Awhile this beauty captivated,
1969	And that no longer could inspire.
1970	Slowly exhausted by desire,
1971	Yet satiated with success,
1972	In solitude or worldly din,
1973	He heard his soul's complaint within,
1974	With laughter smothered weariness:
1975	And thus he spent eight years of time,
1976	Destroyed the blossom of his prime.
1977	Though beauty he no more adored,
1978	He still made love in a queer way;
1979	Rebuffed--as quickly reassured,
1980	Jilted--glad of a holiday.
1981	Without enthusiasm he met
1982	The fair, nor parted with regret,
1983	Scarce mindful of their love and guile.
1984	Thus a guest with composure will
1985	To take a hand at whist oft come:
1986	He takes his seat, concludes his game,
1987	And straight returning whence he came,
1988	Tranquilly goes to sleep at home,
1989	And in the morning doth not know
1990	Whither that evening he will go.
1991	However, Tania's letter reading,
1992	Eugene was touched with sympathy;
1993	The language of her girlish pleading
1994	Aroused in him sweet reverie.
1995	He called to mind Tattiana's grace,
1996	Pallid and melancholy face,
1997	And in a vision, sinless, bright,
1998	His spirit sank with strange delight.
1999	May be the empire of the sense,
2000	Regained authority awhile,
2001	But he desired not to beguile
2002	Such open-hearted innocence.
2003	But to the garden once again
2004	Wherein we lately left the twain.
2005	Two minutes they in silence spent,
2006	Oneguine then approached and said:
2007	"You have a letter to me sent.
2008	Do not excuse yourself. I read
2009	Confessions which a trusting heart
2010	May well in innocence impart.
2011	Charming is your sincerity,
2012	Feelings which long had ceased to be
2013	It wakens in my breast again.
2014	But I came not to adulate:
2015	Your frankness I shall compensate
2016	By an avowal just as plain.
2017	An ear to my confession lend;
2018	To thy decree my will I bend.
2019	"If the domestic hearth could bless--
2020	My sum of happiness contained;
2021	If wife and children to possess
2022	A happy destiny ordained:
2023	If in the scenes of home I might
2024	E'en for an instant find delight,
2025	Then, I say truly, none but thee
2026	I would desire my bride to be--
2027	I say without poetic phrase,
2028	Found the ideal of my youth,
2029	Thee only would I choose, in truth,
2030	As partner of my mournful days,
2031	Thee only, pledge of all things bright,
2032	And be as happy--as I might.
2033	"But strange am I to happiness;
2034	'Tis foreign to my cast of thought;
